  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_cc8cc4e9-7f2d-43a1-a1a1-98afc42d92a7'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };

      initAppleId();

      initializeSiwa();

Popular Answered Questions

<div id='communityQuestionsWithAnswers'>
<div class='communityQuestionAndAnswer'>
<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/1118509-can-the-jeeves-novels-and-short-stories-be">Can the Jeeves novels and short stories be read out of order?</a>
</div>
<div class='communityQAFooter questionFooter'>
<ul class='questionFooterActions'>
<li class='footerItem like'>
<span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_community_question_1118509'><a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F16241200-thank-you-jeeves">like</a></span>

</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionTimestamp'>
<a href="/questions/1118509-can-the-jeeves-novels-and-short-stories-be">3 years ago</a>
</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionPermalink'>
<a href="/questions/1118509-can-the-jeeves-novels-and-short-stories-be">See all 3 answers</a>
</li>
</ul>
</div>

</div>
<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/7065529-mitali"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1460721630i/7065529._UX100_CR0,13,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/7065529-mitali">Mitali</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">Yes. There are allusions to events of previous books, and several characters reappear in the books. But the characters are re-introduced in every book<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">Yes. There are allusions to events of previous books, and several characters reappear in the books. But the characters are re-introduced in every book they appear, so you're unlikely to be confused about who they are. <br /><br />There are one or two cases in which the ending of a book is referred to in later books, which some people might consider spoilers. But given that these stories are not mysteries (far from it), knowing the ending really shouldn't have any effect on your enjoyment of the books. Still, if you're extremely wary of spoilers, better read in publication order.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>
</div>

<div id="review_265138905" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/265138905" />
    <a title="Henry Avila" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5431458-henry-avila"><img alt="Henry Avila" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1307597403p2/5431458.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/265138905?book_show_action=true">Jan 20, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Henry Avila" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Henry Avila" href="/user/show/5431458-henry-avila">Henry Avila</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781585674343" href="/book/show/16392.Thank_You_Jeeves">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer265138905" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13692341309209068686">Bertram "Bertie" Wooster is part of the English idle rich, don't you wish you were too ? Set during the bleak Great Depression of the 1930's, these dark aspects are unseen by the clueless gentleman; that strangely gives it charm... Most of his friends are members of the notorious Drones Club in London and hardly notice the bad economic conditions either, such a bunch of nincompoops the world would be hard to find anymore. He has a valet Jeeves, much smarter than his boss and for that matter his </span>
  <span id="freeText13692341309209068686" style="display:none">Bertram "Bertie" Wooster is part of the English idle rich, don't you wish you were too ? Set during the bleak Great Depression of the 1930's, these dark aspects are unseen by the clueless gentleman; that strangely gives it charm... Most of his friends are members of the notorious Drones Club in London and hardly notice the bad economic conditions either, such a bunch of nincompoops the world would be hard to find anymore. He has a valet Jeeves, much smarter than his boss and for that matter his friends. That comes in handy, as Jeeves is always getting them out of trouble, with small assistance from his employer. The big mystery is why such an obvious genius remains, I know times are tough yet with the constant harassment and never ending turmoil; but maybe this will change... The often engaged but never married Bertie, finally drives poor Jeeves out, by Wooster's incessant banjo playing; excruciating caterwauling which would raise the dead... even butlers have limits. Leaving the noisy city and going to live in a quiet country cottage by the sea, where there are no neighbors to complain about, shall we say Bertie's not a very expert performer, being kind . The silly Lord "Chuffy"Chuffnell the landlord and one of Wooster's numerous Oxford friends, needs help, plenty of it. Miss Pauline Stoker , Bertie's ex- fiancee (for two days) and new love of Chuffy's , the lord doesn't know about their past peccadilloes and Bertie can keep a secret, is visiting here, yes trouble will commence shortly... Her wealthy American father has brought his little yacht, to keep an eye on the pretty but fickle athletic daughter , Jeeves new employer is you guessed it Lord Chuffnell. The fun begins with the eccentric Bertie (the poor are called crazy) and his new mysterious valet Brinkley, cause the local police the two of them , many headaches migraines if truth be told. The forever calm Jeeves will have to use all his wisdom to eliminate the misunderstandings , for a hopeful, proper and acceptable ending . This is England....after all.....A delightful amusing romp into the mythical lives of the very well- to- do, there adventures, problems and mishaps admittedly not serious, compared to us, still quite entertaining as written by the incomparable Mr.... excuse me, Sir P.G. ( Pelham Grenville) Wodehouse even his name brings a smile to the reader.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13692341309209068686" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3027246669" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3027246669" />
    <a title="Evgeny" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9340136-evgeny"><img alt="Evgeny" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1544797852p2/9340136.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3027246669?book_show_action=true">Nov 02, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Evgeny" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Evgeny" href="/user/show/9340136-evgeny">Evgeny</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9340136-evgeny?shelf=humor">humor</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3027246669" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13653528726375112844"><i>A guy told his friend, "Can you imagine the nerve of my neighbor? He was knocking on my wall non-stop at midnight. Luckily I was not asleep at the time: I was playing trombone".</i><br/><br/>The joke above is very appropriate as the book starts with Bertie Wooster trying to learn to play banjo. <br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1572921961i/28403020._SX540_.jpg' alt='Banjo' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/>Not surprisingly the result was his neighbors complaining (see the joke above; some people have no appreciation for music) and his landlord kicking the poor guy out of his flat. <br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1572922031i/28403021._SX540_.jpg' alt='Kick out' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/>Bertie decided to go to a countryside wh</span>
  <span id="freeText13653528726375112844" style="display:none"><i>A guy told his friend, "Can you imagine the nerve of my neighbor? He was knocking on my wall non-stop at midnight. Luckily I was not asleep at the time: I was playing trombone".</i><br><br>The joke above is very appropriate as the book starts with Bertie Wooster trying to learn to play banjo. <br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1572921961i/28403020._SX540_.jpg" alt="Banjo" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>Not surprisingly the result was his neighbors complaining (see the joke above; some people have no appreciation for music) and his landlord kicking the poor guy out of his flat. <br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1572922031i/28403021._SX540_.jpg" alt="Kick out" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>Bertie decided to go to a countryside where neighbors are far enough to endure his attempts at torturing both banjo and listeners. Here came the biggest Bertie's problem: Jeeves decided he did not like countryside, especially with music provided by Bertie, so he quit. <br><br>In search of a vacant countryside house Bertie stumbled upon his childhood friend Chuffy. The latter provided a house, but a complications arose. Chuffy was madly in love with Pauline Stoker, an American whose father just inherited several million dollars. <br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1572922154i/28403027._SX540_.jpg" alt="Money" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>Pauline happened to be Bertie's ex-fiance so our hero decided to help the couple to get along. He meddled and the result was the usual unmanageable mess of affairs. <br><br><i>“I mean, if you're asking a fellow to come out of a room so that you can dismember him with a carving knife, it's absurd to tack a 'sir' on to every sentence. The two things don't go together.”</i><br><br>Only this time there was no Jeeves to help untangle it. Luckily not all was lost as Chuffy snatched Jeeves the moment he became unemployed. <br><br>The biggest problem with the book I found: Bertie sans Jeeves does not work. Sure, his outlook at life is refreshing and always amusing, but it takes a cool character like Jeeves to counterbalance him and create outrageously funny situations. <br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1572922683i/28403043._SX540_.jpg" width="300" height="230" alt="Butler" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>I found almost none here. In my humble opinion if you discount the initial short stories with the pair, the book was the lest funny of the series so far. 3 stars is as high as I am going to give. <br><br>Please take a note that for some people the book could appear to have racist undertones as it has several white people blacken their faces to pass for a people of different race. To this I can only say, "Hey, if it was OK and good for a modern Canadian Prime Minister it should definitely present no problem for a book written almost 100 years ago". Case dismissed. </span>
  <a data-text-id="13653528726375112844" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_14801858" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/14801858" />
    <a title="Dan Schwent" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/870755-dan-schwent"><img alt="Dan Schwent" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1593973948p2/870755.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/14801858?book_show_action=true">Feb 07, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dan Schwent" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dan Schwent" href="/user/show/870755-dan-schwent">Dan Schwent</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781585674343" href="/book/show/16392.Thank_You_Jeeves">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/870755-dan-schwent?shelf=2012">2012</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/870755-dan-schwent?shelf=wodehouse">wodehouse</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer14801858" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6273735899140936137">Jeeves and Wooster break up after Jeeves lays down an ultimatum and Bertie chooses his banjolele over his manservant. Hilarity ensues.<br/><br/><b>The 2011-2012 re-read...</b><br/>After Jeeves and Wooster have a spat over a banjolele, Jeeves leaves Wooster for Lord Chuffnel, who is enamored with Bertie's ex-fiancee, Pauline Stoker. Complicating matters are Stoker's dad, a millionaire who wants to buy Chuffnel's mansion, and Chuffy's, who is being pursued by Wooster's old nemesis, Sir Roderick Glossop. When Bertie win</span>
  <span id="freeText6273735899140936137" style="display:none">Jeeves and Wooster break up after Jeeves lays down an ultimatum and Bertie chooses his banjolele over his manservant. Hilarity ensues.<br /><br /><b>The 2011-2012 re-read...</b><br />After Jeeves and Wooster have a spat over a banjolele, Jeeves leaves Wooster for Lord Chuffnel, who is enamored with Bertie's ex-fiancee, Pauline Stoker. Complicating matters are Stoker's dad, a millionaire who wants to buy Chuffnel's mansion, and Chuffy's, who is being pursued by Wooster's old nemesis, Sir Roderick Glossop. When Bertie winds up engaged to Pauline instead of Chuffy, can even Jeeves set things right?<br /><br />After so many books, I'm almost at a loss at trying to convey the awesomeness of P.G. Wodehouse in words. He moves the characters through the various scenes like a puppet master. While this is the first Jeeves novel, it's by no means the genius butler's first outing. I'd forgotten how much history there was between Bertie and Sir Roderick Glossop, renowned nerve specialist, prior to this book. The supporting cast did a good job driving the story, from the Stokers to the Chuffnels to Brinkley, the replacement Jeeves. Little Seabury and his protection racket cracked me up. <br /><br />Things are not as perfect as they would later become in the Jeeves books. Today's readers may find Bertie and Glossop being in blackface for a good portion of the story and the casual use of the n-word is sure to offend. Since this was the first full length Jeeves outing, it feels like old Plum was still working some of the bugs out.<br /><br />Still, it's still a great story. So many lines of quotable dialogue and hilarious situations. Even though you can see a lot of them coming, it doesn't lessen the impact. As usual, it was a joy to watch Wodehouse do what he does best.<br /><br />I enjoyed revisiting Thank You, Jeeves, with the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/group/show/645" rel="nofollow noopener">Wodehouse Cracks Me Up</a> group and am excited about reading the other eight this year.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6273735899140936137" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3023563717" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3023563717" />
    <a title="Anne" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1720620-anne"><img alt="Anne" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1415374035p2/1720620.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3023563717?book_show_action=true">Oct 24, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Anne" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Anne" href="/user/show/1720620-anne">Anne</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audiobook 9781572704602" href="/book/show/105984.Thank_You_Jeeves">review of another edition</a>

            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1720620-anne?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1720620-anne?shelf=humor">humor</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3023563717" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9053782940003612951"><b>Not everyone appreciates the banjolele.</b><br/><br/> <img src='https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1574949332ra/28523905.gif' width='350' height='275' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>Sadly for Bertie Wooster, <i>Jeeves</i> is among those who are quite fed up with his newest hobby. After getting hit with a noise complaint from his neighbors and being deserted by his butler, Bertie heads out to the country to continue to strum his instrument.<br/>Of course, everything happens to make that utterly impossible as he gets caught up in one hysterical mishap after another.<br/><br/> <img src='https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1575212616ra/28540348.gif' width='350' height='275' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>This is one of the funnier <b>Jeeves</b> titles and definitely worth reading is you're a f</span>
  <span id="freeText9053782940003612951" style="display:none"><b>Not everyone appreciates the banjolele.</b><br><br> <img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1574949332ra/28523905.gif" width="350" height="275" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Sadly for Bertie Wooster, <i>Jeeves</i> is among those who are quite fed up with his newest hobby. After getting hit with a noise complaint from his neighbors and being deserted by his butler, Bertie heads out to the country to continue to strum his instrument.<br>Of course, everything happens to make that utterly impossible as he gets caught up in one hysterical mishap after another.<br><br> <img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1575212616ra/28540348.gif" width="350" height="275" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>This is one of the funnier <b>Jeeves</b> titles and definitely worth reading is you're a fan of Wodehouse, or even just looking for a humorous book.<br><b>BUT.</b><br>I <i>do</i> feel that you should be warned that there's a fair bit of really cringy shit in this one that kind of puts the brakes on the funny for a good bit while you try to recover from the shock of the causal racism. I mean, if you read a lot of older novels, you'll be pretty well used to quite a lot of it.<br>You don't have to like it to admit that it just pops up everywhere.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1575213286ra/28540377.gif" width="350" height="250" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>But between the N-word, the whole escape plan hinging on blackface, and the maids screaming &amp; fainting when they saw Bertie? And just the simple idea people were so stupid to think that some English dude who has shoe polish or whatever on their face could actually pass for someone of a different ethnicity?<br>It was so insulting. And it just went on <i>forever</i>.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1575213286ra/28540378.gif" width="350" height="250" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Now, <i>maybe</i> the author was trying to use this as a way to show how ridiculous people could act over something as simple as skin color. Bertie was the same person underneath it all, but he garnered very different reactions when he pulled his little switcheroo. <br>Unfortunately, Wodehouse is dead, so it's not like we can ask him. <br>And as much as I hate to admit it, this is still a hilarious story...<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1575214021ra/28540417.gif" width="350" height="250" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>This would actually be a 5 star book without all the weird racism, but it seriously put a damper on my enjoyment, so I gotta knock off a star even if Wodehouse didn't realize what he was doing.<br><br><b>Recommended for Wodehouse fans and Bertie &amp; Jeeves addicts.</b><br><br>Jonathan Cecil - Narrator</span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jason Koivu" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jason Koivu" href="/user/show/1742824-jason-koivu">Jason Koivu</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781585674343" href="/book/show/16392.Thank_You_Jeeves">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1742824-jason-koivu?shelf=comedy">comedy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1742824-jason-koivu?shelf=humor">humor</a>,

              <span id="reviewTextContainer38451431" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3879890695373561778">More tales of woe for Bertie Wooster, but this time he's on his own with no Jeeves to get him out of the soup. <br/><br/>Perhaps that's why I felt this volume - though probably as good as the rest - didn't quite resonate the way others have. Wooster without Jeeves is like Laurel without Hardy, Abbot sans Costello. The reason these books work is that they are a duo, they play off of one another. Wooster needs Jeeves, and in a way, Jeeves needs Wooster...at least for comedic purposes. <br/><br/>But the good thing abo</span>
  <span id="freeText3879890695373561778" style="display:none">More tales of woe for Bertie Wooster, but this time he's on his own with no Jeeves to get him out of the soup. <br /><br />Perhaps that's why I felt this volume - though probably as good as the rest - didn't quite resonate the way others have. Wooster without Jeeves is like Laurel without Hardy, Abbot sans Costello. The reason these books work is that they are a duo, they play off of one another. Wooster needs Jeeves, and in a way, Jeeves needs Wooster...at least for comedic purposes. <br /><br />But the good thing about reading Wodehouse is that it's like watching a rerun of an old favorite tv show: You can anticipate all the jokes, but you laugh anyway. So although the usual winning formula goes a bit cockeyed in <i>Thank You, Jeeves</i> there's still enough Wodehouse-isms to keep his fans happy.</span>

<div id="review_58783460" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/58783460" />
    <a title="Amanda" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1219253-amanda"><img alt="Amanda" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1411956191p2/1219253.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/58783460?book_show_action=true">Jun 07, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Amanda" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Amanda" href="/user/show/1219253-amanda">Amanda</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781585674343" href="/book/show/16392.Thank_You_Jeeves">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1219253-amanda?shelf=blog">blog</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer58783460" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14725672121821881359">For the past couple of years, the name P. G. Wodehouse kept popping up in interviews and articles about some of my favorite people (most notably Hugh Laurie and Neil Gaiman, among others). They praised him as THE master of British comedy. Since I admittedly like my comedy British, I decided it was time to give Wodehouse a try. <br/><br/>The thing with Wodehouse is that he creeps up on you. During the first few chapters, I thought, "What's all the fuss about?" There is some admittedly clever language and t</span>
  <span id="freeText14725672121821881359" style="display:none">For the past couple of years, the name P. G. Wodehouse kept popping up in interviews and articles about some of my favorite people (most notably Hugh Laurie and Neil Gaiman, among others). They praised him as THE master of British comedy. Since I admittedly like my comedy British, I decided it was time to give Wodehouse a try. <br /><br />The thing with Wodehouse is that he creeps up on you. During the first few chapters, I thought, "What's all the fuss about?" There is some admittedly clever language and the strange turn of phrase, but nothing laugh out loud hilarious. In true Wooster fashion, I thought, "Everyone who loves this man must have gone potty. They're seriously off their onion. What a rummy bit of business this is." And then it happened--a smile here, a titter there, a giggle, and then laugh out loud hilarity. Particularly hilarious are Wooster's attempts to go to sleep while being constantly awakened by the far too diligent local lawmen, Jeeves' plan to smuggle Wooster off the yacht where he's being held captive (which results in Wooster spending a good portion of the book in black face), the quest for slabs of butter, and the maniacal replacement for Jeeves (who quit Wooster's employ because of his disdain for the banjolele). <br /><br />Many of the jokes aren't subtle in that you know exactly how one event leads to the creation of a particularly vexing problem for our man Wooster. However, that doesn't rob the book of its fun as the anticipation of the event lends itself to a certain joyful giddiness when the events do indeed come to pass.<br /><br />Cross posted at <a href="http://viiamanda.blogspot.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">This Insignificant Cinder</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="14725672121821881359" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1688461300" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1688461300" />
    <a title="Algernon (Darth Anyan)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4728718-algernon-darth-anyan"><img alt="Algernon (Darth Anyan)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1553101944p2/4728718.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1688461300?book_show_action=true">Jul 05, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Algernon (Darth Anyan)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Algernon (Darth Anyan)" href="/user/show/4728718-algernon-darth-anyan">Algernon (Darth Anyan)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4728718-algernon-darth-anyan?shelf=2016">2016</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1688461300" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9164520839769059626">[9/10]<br/><br/><br/>In one of the scaliest threats to his bachelor state, our old acquaintance Bertram Wooster is left without the moral support of his dependable gentleman's gentleman Jeeves. It all starts with a misunderstanding about a banjolele. On one side, Bertie is convinced that assiduous practice makes perfect when learning to play his newest toy. On the other, his neighbours have given him an ultimatum - either he or his bajolele must go from his posh London residence. Jeeves joins the ranks of the </span>
  <span id="freeText9164520839769059626" style="display:none">[9/10]<br /><br /><br />In one of the scaliest threats to his bachelor state, our old acquaintance Bertram Wooster is left without the moral support of his dependable gentleman's gentleman Jeeves. It all starts with a misunderstanding about a banjolele. On one side, Bertie is convinced that assiduous practice makes perfect when learning to play his newest toy. On the other, his neighbours have given him an ultimatum - either he or his bajolele must go from his posh London residence. Jeeves joins the ranks of the malcontents and hands in his resignation.<br /><br />Rather than give up his musical pursuits, Bertie Wooster stiffs the upper lip, releases Jeeves and chooses self-exile to the countryside in Chuffnell Regis, the residential seat of his old school pal 'Chuffy', the fifth baron Chuffnell of Chuffnell Hall <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ and known only to his recent fiancee by his given name, Marmaduke  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.<br /><br />Instead of the peace and quiet he hoped for, Bertie finds himself thrown once again in the soup, this time courtesy of his own reckless behaviour rather than under the push of an imperious aunt. As usual with P G Wodehouse, these troubles refer mostly to matters of the heart. Something in the air surrounding these stately English manors makes people <i> 'suddenly begin to leap about like a lamb in the springtime.' </i> and contemplate marriage. Chuffy has his eyes on the lovely Pauline Stoker, formerly briefly engaged to Bertie while on a visit to New York. Chuffy's mother, the Lady Chuffnell feels the stirrings of a late spring when gazing into the eyes of Sir Roderick Glossop, another character familiar from past misadventures. The scion of Chuffnell Hall, 'young blighted Seabury', plays an unlikely Cupid who prefers to make some money instead of firing arrows at loving hearts (<i>'Things happen to guys that don't kick in their protection money,' he said dreamily.</i>)<br /><br />&gt;&gt;&lt;&lt;&gt;&gt;&lt;&lt;&gt;&gt;&lt;&lt;<br /><br />After a couple of short story collections in the series I was well content to explore the kind of mayhem P G Wodehouse is capable of when using the longer form of narration. The basic structure of the plot remains familiar, but the potential for trouble is multiplied tenfold, especially when you throw in a pair of heliotrope pyjamas, a furious father hellbent on a shotgun wedding, a replacement butler (Brinkley) with a fondness for strong drink and for starting the socialist revolution in the Wooster cottage, a double serving of boot polish aggravated by an unexpected penury of butter, a couple of earnest village coppers with a bad case of insomnia and a breakfast plate of kippered herrings that seem as hard to win as the South Pole. (I am trying to be as vague as possible mentioning the key ingredients of the farce without spoiling the actual events)<br /><br />It seems like a case that can be solved only by the oversized brain of Jeeves, so lets hope he and Bertie deal with the banjolele issue before wedding bells start to ring for the young Wooster.<br /><br /><br /><b> Thank You, Jeeves </b> is an entertaining addition to the Jeeves &amp; Wooster canon, and I highly recommend it as the perfect companion to a rainy day:<br /><br /><i> It seems almost impossible, considering what the night life of Chuffnell Regis was like, but the next thing that woke me was not a girl leaping from under the bed, her father bounding in with blood in his eye, or a police sergeant playing ragtime on the knocker, but actually birds outside the window heralding in a new day. </i><br /><br />&gt;&gt;&lt;&lt;&gt;&gt;&lt;&lt;&gt;&gt;&lt;&lt;<br /><br />endnotes: Mr. Wodehouse had me once again joyfully diving for the dictionary as he explores the less trodden paths of the English language:<br /><br />- to asseverate = to affirm or declare positively or earnestly, to affirm positively<br />- zareba = an improvised stockade constructed in parts of Africa especially of thorny bushes<br /><br />More, please!<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9164520839769059626" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2795003488" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

  <span id="freeText4977832684836517273" style="display:none"><b>For gentle humor you can't beat Jeeves and Wooster in this novel sized story. <br />P.C. Warning! Contains non P.C. language.</b><br /><br /><i>Such great recall</i><br />'Jeeves,' I recollect saying, on returning to the apartment, 'who was the fellow who on looking at something felt like somebody looking at something? I learned the passage at school, but it has escaped me.'<br /><br />'I fancy the individual you have in mind, sir, is the poet Keats, who compared his emotions on first reading Chapman's Homer to those of stout Cortez when with eagle eyes he stared at the Pacific'<br /><br /><i>A small enemy</i><br />Little Seabury, a child who should have been strangled at birth. I have no positive proof, but I have always been convinced that it was he who put the lizard in my bed the last time I stayed at the Hall.<br /><br /><i>His new valet longs for the revolution</i><br />Outwardly he was all respectfulness, but inwardly you could see that he was a man who was musing on the coming Social Revolution and looked on Bertram as a tyrant and an oppressor.<br />'Yes, Brinkley, I shall dine out.'<br />He said nothing, merely looking at me as if he were measuring me for my lamp-post.<br />He sighed slightly. All this talk of my going to shows was distressing him. What he really wanted was to see me sprinting down Park Lane with the mob after me with dripping knives.<br /><br /><i>Don't tell a lady to calm down</i><br />'Bertie.'<br />'Hallo?'<br />'Ever been hit over the head with a chair?'<br />'No.'<br />'Well, you soon may be.'<br />I began to see she was in difficult mood.<br /><br /><i>Its the usual trope of "who will marry whom" with Bertie getting caught in the middle</i><br />'What are your views upon engagements, Mr Wooster?'<br />'Engagements?'<br />'Should they be short or long?'<br />'Well ...'<br />'I prefer them short. I feel that we had best put this wedding through as quickly as possible. I shall have to find out how soon that is on this side. I believe you cannot simply go to the nearest minister, as in my country. There are formalities. While these are being attended to, you will, of course, be my guest. I'm afraid I can't offer you the freedom of the boat, because you are a pretty slippery young gentleman and might suddenly remember a date elsewhere – some unfortunate appointment which would necessitate your leaving. But I shall do my best to make you comfortable in this room for the next few days.<br /><br /><i>By the end Bertie and all his companions are in a terrible muddle with business and marriage plans threatened with failure.<br />And like a deus ex machina Jeeves enters and all is solved. A most satisfactory ending.</i><br /><br /><br />Enjoy!<br /><br /><br /><br /></span>

    <a title="Nigeyb" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10301421-nigeyb"><img alt="Nigeyb" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1593065725p2/10301421.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1293275118?book_show_action=true">May 29, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nigeyb" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nigeyb" href="/user/show/10301421-nigeyb">Nigeyb</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099496304" href="/book/show/1134038.Thank_You_Jeeves">review of another edition</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1293275118" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7875252499854196206"><b>Sublime, splendid, superb.…in short, PG perfection</b><br/><br/><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/7963.P_G__Wodehouse' title='P.G. Wodehouse' rel='nofollow noopener'>P.G. Wodehouse</a> is the funniest writer of the past century. Wodehouse defies superlatives. He is, quite simply, the best comedic writer to ever put pen to paper. I am a confirmed Wodehousian and revel in the man’s comedic genius. I have read numerous books by the great man and all, to one degree or another, are a delight. <br/><br/>I read <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16241200.Thank_You__Jeeves__Jeeves___5_' title='Thank You, Jeeves (Jeeves, #5) by P.G. Wodehouse' rel='nofollow noopener'>Thank You, Jeeves</a> for my book group and, once I had started, I realised this was the third time I’d read it. It was like</span>
  <span id="freeText7875252499854196206" style="display:none"><b>Sublime, splendid, superb.…in short, PG perfection</b><br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/7963.P_G__Wodehouse" title="P.G. Wodehouse" rel="nofollow noopener">P.G. Wodehouse</a> is the funniest writer of the past century. Wodehouse defies superlatives. He is, quite simply, the best comedic writer to ever put pen to paper. I am a confirmed Wodehousian and revel in the man’s comedic genius. I have read numerous books by the great man and all, to one degree or another, are a delight. <br /><br />I read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16241200.Thank_You__Jeeves__Jeeves___5_" title="Thank You, Jeeves (Jeeves, #5) by P.G. Wodehouse" rel="nofollow noopener">Thank You, Jeeves</a> for my book group and, once I had started, I realised this was the third time I’d read it. It was like meeting an old and valued friend. It is one of my favourites: sublime, splendid, superb…in short, PG perfection.<br /><br />I chuckled, I laughed and I even guffawed. Upon finishing this wonderful book, I discovered that this is the first of the Jeeves and Wooster novels. Up until the publication of this novel, the characters had only appeared in short stories. <br /><br />A rift between Jeeves and Wooster separates our two heroes, Jeeves being unable to stand Bertie’s latest craze - the Banjolele (who knew Banjos were known as Banjoleles?) and so tenders his resignation. <br /><br />Lovelorn characters, a country house, buffoonery, and moronic misunderstandings are all present and correct. The happy ending even bought a tear to my eye. <br /><br />As is so often the case with books written in the early 20th century, the modern reader may look askance at some of the racial epithets that were acceptable at the time however, and whilst I do not for one moment excuse it, sadly it was socially acceptable back in 1934. That important point aside, I say again this is PG perfection.<br /><br />Suffice it to say that, if you don’t know why so many people worship at the comedic altar of PGW, this novel will reveal all.<br /><br />5/5</span>
  <a data-text-id="7875252499854196206" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3558651429" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3558651429" />
    <a title="Paul  Perry" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4107783-paul-perry"><img alt="Paul  Perry" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1606145814p2/4107783.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3558651429?book_show_action=true">Sep 28, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul  Perry" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul  Perry" href="/user/show/4107783-paul-perry">Paul  Perry</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4107783-paul-perry?shelf=read-in-2020">read-in-2020</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4107783-paul-perry?shelf=male-author">male-author</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4107783-paul-perry?shelf=british-author">british-author</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4107783-paul-perry?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4107783-paul-perry?shelf=humour">humour</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4107783-paul-perry?shelf=20th-century">20th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4107783-paul-perry?shelf=english">english</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4107783-paul-perry?shelf=comic">comic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4107783-paul-perry?shelf=comedy">comedy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4107783-paul-perry?shelf=english-author">english-author</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3558651429" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3336270543197352883">What is there to say about Wodehouse? Certainly one of the wittiest writers in the English language, a master of constructing a plot and piercing snobbery whilst reinforcing the systems that drive it. I had dipped in to Jeeves and Wooster before, but wanted to read the canon. My intention had been to read them in order, but on seeing the first two omnibus volumes on sale on kindle went with that. In order, the first volume contains #5, #7 and #2, for which I am sure there is some logic.<br/><br/><br/><br/>Thank You</span>
  <span id="freeText3336270543197352883" style="display:none">What is there to say about Wodehouse? Certainly one of the wittiest writers in the English language, a master of constructing a plot and piercing snobbery whilst reinforcing the systems that drive it. I had dipped in to Jeeves and Wooster before, but wanted to read the canon. My intention had been to read them in order, but on seeing the first two omnibus volumes on sale on kindle went with that. In order, the first volume contains #5, #7 and #2, for which I am sure there is some logic.<br /><br /><br /><br />Thank You, Jeeves begins with Bertie falling out with both his neighbours in his mansion block and his trusted manservant after being infected with a passion for playing the banjolele. He retreats to the country, to stay in a cottage on the estate of his old friend Chuffy to practice his music in peace, only to be descended upon by a beautiful American heiress to whom he had briefly been engaged, her father (who bears no love for Bertram) and the man who had been responsible for scuppering the affair. Cue witticisms, misunderstandings, idiocies, a rather unsuitable replacement for Jeeves, kidnapping and minstrels.<br /><br /><br /><br />Oh yes. The minstrels.<br /><br /><br /><br />While the music troupe do not make an appearance in person, they are referred to and become a major plot point. And herein lies a big problem with this novel.<br /><br /><br /><br />Bear in mind, this was published in 1933 by an upper class, white (those were and possibly still are the only kind of Upper Class) Englishman, and we have to keep in mind that we should read things in view of when and where they were written, however then Bertie begins to casually refer to the "n***** minstrels" it was a cold slap in the face. This isn't Twain knowingly using the word fifty years earlier, although I realise most (white) people wouldn't have thought it as poisonous as it is today. Jeeves uses the "politer" version of the word, although I think that is simply to compare his propriety to Bertie's slang-laden speech.<br /><br /><br /><br />The inclusion of the minstrels (in absentia though it is) is merely to allow two instances of blackface later in the book - one for purposes of deception, another for entertainment. Whether inured - or numbed - by the language, this only caused me to roll my eyes, but also made me think how much part of British comedic culture this was until amazingly recently. I grew up in the seventies and eighties and racist stereotypes, including black-, yellow- and brownface, were so common in "light entertainment" as to be unremarkable. The Black and White Minstrel Show ran - stunningly - until 1980 on British TV. The <i>Carry On</i> films, <i>It Ain't Half Hot, Mum</i>, <i>Mind Your Language</i> were all influenced by this strand of British colonial racism.<br /><br /><br /><br />So, I repeat to myself "it was a different time!" and am thankful that things have changed so much. I hope this doesn't put me off continuing with J&amp;W but fervently also hope that such things aren't repeated, and I grit my teeth and mark the book on the merits and qualities and award it 4 stars.<br /><br /><br /><br />OK, maybe 3.5.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3336270543197352883" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1959193572" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1959193572" />
    <a title="Lou" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/61573845-lou"><img alt="Lou" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1602176070p2/61573845.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1959193572?book_show_action=true">May 26, 2017</a>

<div id="review_254923179" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/254923179" />
    <a title=" ~Geektastic~" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5006157-geektastic"><img alt=" ~Geektastic~" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605801004p2/5006157.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/254923179?book_show_action=true">Jan 02, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title=" ~Geektastic~" class="user" itemprop="url" name=" ~Geektastic~" href="/user/show/5006157-geektastic"> ~Geektastic~</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781585674343" href="/book/show/16392.Thank_You_Jeeves">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5006157-geektastic?shelf=20th-century-classics">20th-century-classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5006157-geektastic?shelf=comedy">comedy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5006157-geektastic?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5006157-geektastic?shelf=2012">2012</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer254923179" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4432357294207132"> PG Wodehouse has been on my list of authors to read for ages, and my only complaint is that I waited so long to give him a try. <br/><br/>Before picking up <i>Thank You, Jeeves</i>, I had read several of the short stories that introduced the world to the indomitable literary pairing of Bertram Wooster and his faithful valet Jeeves. From the first page of the earliest story, “Extricating Old Gussie,” I knew I had found a series that would give me endless hours of cozy, friendly entertainment in the months and ye</span>
  <span id="freeText4432357294207132" style="display:none"> PG Wodehouse has been on my list of authors to read for ages, and my only complaint is that I waited so long to give him a try. <br /><br />Before picking up <i>Thank You, Jeeves</i>, I had read several of the short stories that introduced the world to the indomitable literary pairing of Bertram Wooster and his faithful valet Jeeves. From the first page of the earliest story, “Extricating Old Gussie,” I knew I had found a series that would give me endless hours of cozy, friendly entertainment in the months and years to come. Some books are meant to prod you into deep reflections, or examine “big” problems, but this is certainly not one of those. <i>Thank You, Jeeves</i> is like a homemade quilt and a cup of hot chocolate in the middle of winter, infinitely comfortable and comforting. <br /><br />After appearing in multiple short stories, beginning in 1915, Bertie and Jeeves made their jump to a full-length adventure in <i>Thank You, Jeeves</i> (1934). Wodehouse has introduced several delightful characters in his stories, like Uncle Fred and the folks at Blandings Castle, but I think most people would agree that the “Jeeves and Wooster” stories are his ultimate legacy. Bertie, young, scatterbrained and a bit clueless, is perfectly complimented by the eternally cool-headed and logical Jeeves.<br /><br />In this early installment of a series that would span nearly 40 years, Bertie has taken up playing the banjolele, much to the discomfort of his neighbors. Unfortunately, his generally unflappable valet, Jeeves, is similarly discomposed by his master’s new hobby and leaves poor Bertie, a permanent bachelor and not the most self-sufficient man about town, all alone. To escape the persecution of his aurally assaulted neighbors, Bertie decides to rent a cottage in the country from a good friend, the landed but poor Lord Chuffnell, Chuffy for short. A chance meeting of several characters from earlier stories, a run-in with the local law enforcement, an unfortunate application of boot polish and some lady trouble all add up to a hilarious adventure, with enough convenient but perfectly structured twists to keep you guessing (and laughing). I won’t give it away, but anyone who has ever read anything about Bertie and Jeeves knows who comes to the rescue in the end.<br /><br />Despite the comedic situations and overall light tone of his work, don’t be deceived into thinking Wodehouse is deficient in serious talent. He may not be interested in the profound, but his plots are structures to be reckoned with, brilliantly layering trouble upon trouble without ever leaving a loose end or pointless observation. He could seriously give Arthur Conan Doyle a run for his money, and make you laugh so hard you never notice all the work he has put into it. Likewise, his ability to use perfect dialog and very little description to create memorable characters is no small feat. It’s a testament to his ability that Jeeves has so frequently been reincarnated in popular culture, in TV shows like “Benson” and “The Fresh Prince of Bel Air.”<br /><br />The Bertie and Jeeves stories are, essentially, the marshmallow fluff of literature, and while you can’t survive long on a diet exclusively of sweet, fluffy goodness, life would be dull and, well, fluffless without it. And a life without fluff is no life at all. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4432357294207132" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2241413636" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer13981223156493732072">EXCERPT: About three months before, noting a certain liveliness in my Aunt Agatha, I had deemed it prudent to pop across to New York for a space to give her time to blow over. And about halfway through my first week there, in the course of a beano of some description at the Sherry-Netherland, I made the acquaintance of Pauline Stoker. <br/><br/>She got right in among me. Her beauty maddened me like wine. <br/><br/>THE BLURB: Thank You, Jeeves is the first novel to feature the incomparable valet Jeeves and his haple</span>
  <span id="freeText13981223156493732072" style="display:none">EXCERPT: About three months before, noting a certain liveliness in my Aunt Agatha, I had deemed it prudent to pop across to New York for a space to give her time to blow over. And about halfway through my first week there, in the course of a beano of some description at the Sherry-Netherland, I made the acquaintance of Pauline Stoker. <br /><br />She got right in among me. Her beauty maddened me like wine. <br /><br />THE BLURB: Thank You, Jeeves is the first novel to feature the incomparable valet Jeeves and his hapless charge Bertie Wooster - and you've hardly started to turn the pages when he resigns over Bertie's dedicated but somewhat untuneful playing of the banjo. In high dudgeon, Bertie disappears to the country as a guest of his chum Chuffy - only to find his peace shattered by the arrival of his ex-fiancée Pauline Stoker, her formidable father and the eminent loony-doctor Sir Roderick Glossop. When Chuffy falls in love with Pauline and Bertie seems to be caught in flagrante, a situation boils up which only Jeeves (whether employed or not) can simmer down...A display of sustained comic brilliance, this novel shows Wodehouse rising to the top of his game.<br /><br />MY THOUGHTS: I have been having a bit of a Wodehouse/Jeeves fest this week. You really can't beat a bit of bumbling Bertie and the dry acerbic wit of Jeeves. The British do the 'comedy of manners' superbly well. I will be listening to more episodes of the series. <br /><br />I listened to the audiobook Thank You, Jeeves recorded live by L. A. Theatre Works via OverDrive. All opinions expressed in this review are entirely my own personal opinions. <div id="review_217915094" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/217915094" />
    <a title="F.R." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1309606-f-r"><img alt="F.R." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1544886612p2/1309606.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/217915094?book_show_action=true">Oct 02, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="F.R." class="user" itemprop="url" name="F.R." href="/user/show/1309606-f-r">F.R.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781585674343" href="/book/show/16392.Thank_You_Jeeves">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer217915094" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4019026678458594495">And so we come to the first of Jeeves and Wooster novels.<br/><br/>I wonder how nervous PG Wodehouse was when he sat down to write this book. After all, here he had characters who had proven their worth in short stories, but would the material really stretch far enough for a whole novel? Could he spin out a plot that would sustain such a length? Was there a danger of the whole thing becoming episodic, a series of short stories joined together? Old P.G. always came across as a jovial and sanguine individua</span>
  <span id="freeText4019026678458594495" style="display:none">And so we come to the first of Jeeves and Wooster novels.<br /><br />I wonder how nervous PG Wodehouse was when he sat down to write this book. After all, here he had characters who had proven their worth in short stories, but would the material really stretch far enough for a whole novel? Could he spin out a plot that would sustain such a length? Was there a danger of the whole thing becoming episodic, a series of short stories joined together? Old P.G. always came across as a jovial and sanguine individual, but when he sat down to write this was there some sweat on the old brow? If so he needn’t have worried. ‘Thank you, Jeeves’ is a delight, which shows Wodehouse at the absolute top of his game.<br /><br />Using the country house formula that he would most definitely make his own, Wodehouse introduces some unfamiliar conflict into the central relationship by having the master and the valet separate over Wooster’s love of banjolele playing. But they’re soon thrown together into a tight locale, with lots of opportunity for Jeeves to pull the young master out of the fire – as they confront an aggressive American millionaire, his flighty daughter, nosey policeman, a psychotic and socialist butler and Sir Roderick Glossop.<br /><br />The 21st century reader may raise an eyebrow at the stuff with the blackface, but then this is a relic of a different time whilst still being fairly timeless (and if we want to go down that judging everything by modern PC standards route, then this would won’t hold up for long – Wodehouse’s female characters will certainly wilt under scrutiny.) What this book offers is comic situations piled onto comic situations, combined with one of the best prose styles in literature. And all one can do is sit back and enjoy the ride and marvel at how smooth it all is.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4019026678458594495" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_51751074" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/51751074" />
    <a title="Steven Harbin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1283483-steven-harbin"><img alt="Steven Harbin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1307406902p2/1283483.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/51751074?book_show_action=true">Apr 06, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steven Harbin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steven Harbin" href="/user/show/1283483-steven-harbin">Steven Harbin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audiobook 9781572704602" href="/book/show/105984.Thank_You_Jeeves">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer51751074&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer51751074" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2726703541207589355">This is first Jeeves and Bertie Wooster novel Wodehouse wrote and was published in 1934. Previously Jeeves appeared only in various short stories. It's a usual mix of humor and mix up and has lots of characters from the stories make a re-appearance, such as Roderick Glossup (a psychiatrist who Bertie often describes as a "looney Doctor") along with beautiful and head strong Pauline Stoker (one of Bertie's numerous former fiancees) and her equally strong willed father, who of course doesn't think</span>
  <span id="freeText2726703541207589355" style="display:none">This is first Jeeves and Bertie Wooster novel Wodehouse wrote and was published in 1934. Previously Jeeves appeared only in various short stories. It's a usual mix of humor and mix up and has lots of characters from the stories make a re-appearance, such as Roderick Glossup (a psychiatrist who Bertie often describes as a "looney Doctor") along with beautiful and head strong Pauline Stoker (one of Bertie's numerous former fiancees) and her equally strong willed father, who of course doesn't think much of Bertie.<br />The crux of the story is that Bertie and Jeeves part ways due to Bertie's latest musical addiction, banjo playing. Jeeves ends up in the employ of Bertie's old classmate Chuffy, while Bertie ends up in a summer cottage on Chuffy's estate, along with his new valet, who is definitely not anything like Jeeves. Before the end, Bertie will be shanghied, engaged against his will, and forced to pretend to be part of a minstrel troupe. <br />An enjoyable story, the only jarring note being the usage of racist terms to describe the mostly off stage minstrel troupe. I realize that the book was in some ways a product of it's time, but hearing Wooster use the "n word" even a couple of times was too much for my modern sensibilities. I gave the book a 4 instead of my usual Wodehouse 5, other works of PGW's are just as humorous and don't have the racist terms.<br />Still, if you are a Jeeves and Wooster fan, you need to read this one, as several characters make their appearance again in later novels.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2726703541207589355" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

Wodehouse you will get free tickets.<br/><br/>The world of Jeeves and Wooster is a frozen slice of English 1920s idyll where people enjoy picnics and play cricket on the village green and people walk out in their Sunday best, the sun always shines and somewhere is the call of a wood pigeon and the sound of a church bell pealing. The worst thing that ever happens is a social faux pas or accidentally getting engaged through farce and misunderstanding.<br/>But don't </span>

The worst thing that ever happens is a social faux pas or accidentally getting engaged through farce and misunderstanding.<br />But don't worry. Jeeves is always at hand to rescue the day with a bit of manipulation.<br /><br />Delightful. Like a warm and comfort blanket.</span>

<div id="review_3237084312" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

<span id="freeTextContainer8136771101603076977">A delightful, humorous, entertaining, well plotted read. Bertie Wooster takes up the playing of the banjolele. Bertie is forced to leave his London apartment due to complaints from other apartment tenants about his playing of the banjolele. Bertie and Jeeves come to an impasse. Jeeves states he cannot continue as Bertie’s employee if Bertie insists on continuing to play the banjolele. Bertie refuses to stop playing the banjolele and Jeeves gives notice. In the country without Jeeves, Bertie find</span>
  <span id="freeText8136771101603076977" style="display:none">A delightful, humorous, entertaining, well plotted read. Bertie Wooster takes up the playing of the banjolele. Bertie is forced to leave his London apartment due to complaints from other apartment tenants about his playing of the banjolele. Bertie and Jeeves come to an impasse. Jeeves states he cannot continue as Bertie’s employee if Bertie insists on continuing to play the banjolele. Bertie refuses to stop playing the banjolele and Jeeves gives notice. In the country without Jeeves, Bertie finds himself caught up in the love tryst between Miss Pauline Stoker and his old school friend, Chuffy. There are some amusing incidents with Bertie having to impersonate a black minstrel by putting on black shoe polish.<br /><br />Here are a some quotes showing P. G. Wodehouse’s sense of fun:<br />‘I mean, if you’re asking a fellow to come out of a room so that you can dismember him with a carving knife, it’s absurd to tack a ‘sir’ on to every sentence. The two things don’t go together.’<br />‘He made a noise like a pig swallowing half a cabbage.’<br />‘You can’t tell me if there are any special subjects to avoid when talking to him, can you? ‘Special subjects?” ‘Well, you know how it is with a stranger. You say it’s a fine day, and he goes all white and tense, because you’ve reminded him that it was on a fine day that his wife eloped with the chauffeur.’</span>
  <a data-text-id="8136771101603076977" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

It is not Elfin. It broods. It says little. It sucks in its breath in a pained way. And every now and again you catch its eye, and it is like colliding with a raw oyster.</i></span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer168471623" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer17045625085370633227">This is the book I enjoyed least of the Jeeves books. If this title was the first one written then the writing has gotten better since fortunately. <em>Thank you, Jeeves</em> was hard to follow, as in, it seemed kind of pointless. I didn't like that the characters dropped the n*bomb quite a few times and wore blackface. It killed the plot for me because it wasn't remotely necessary to the story; only there to provide some comic relief and in poor taste at that. That said, in context it is historical evid</span>
  <span id="freeText17045625085370633227" style="display:none">This is the book I enjoyed least of the Jeeves books. If this title was the first one written then the writing has gotten better since fortunately. <em>Thank you, Jeeves</em> was hard to follow, as in, it seemed kind of pointless. I didn't like that the characters dropped the n*bomb quite a few times and wore blackface. It killed the plot for me because it wasn't remotely necessary to the story; only there to provide some comic relief and in poor taste at that. That said, in context it is historical evidence of the era (1930s) in which this story was written and placed so I'm not for censoring it either.<br /><br />I'd say skip this one.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17045625085370633227" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_321191943" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/321191943" />
    <a title="theduckthief" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1061721-theduckthief"><img alt="theduckthief" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1217650360p2/1061721.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/321191943?book_show_action=true">Apr 29, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="theduckthief" class="user" itemprop="url" name="theduckthief" href="/user/show/1061721-theduckthief">theduckthief</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781585674343" href="/book/show/16392.Thank_You_Jeeves">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer321191943" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1771949379951454107"><b>”You know, the longer I live, the more I feel that the great wheeze in life is to be jolly well sure what you want and not let yourself be put off by pals who think they know better than you do."</b> <br/><br/>Bertie Wooster has become enamoured of the banjolele but is getting nothing but grief from his neighbours and his valet Jeeves. After a severe disagreement about the instrument causes Jeeves to leave his service, Bertie departs for the country to practice in peace. He gets caught up in the affairs of hi</span>
  <span id="freeText1771949379951454107" style="display:none"><b>”You know, the longer I live, the more I feel that the great wheeze in life is to be jolly well sure what you want and not let yourself be put off by pals who think they know better than you do."</b> <br /><br />Bertie Wooster has become enamoured of the banjolele but is getting nothing but grief from his neighbours and his valet Jeeves. After a severe disagreement about the instrument causes Jeeves to leave his service, Bertie departs for the country to practice in peace. He gets caught up in the affairs of his good friend “Chuffy”, current employer of Jeeves, and manages to accidentally get himself engaged to the woman “Chuffy” loves. Madcap hijinks ensue, leaving Bertie to fend for himself without the stalwart advice of his gentleman’s gentleman.<br /><br />This is the second Wodehouse book I’ve read and the first full-length novel. I had high expectations and to my disappointment found that it didn’t measure up to <b>”The Inimitable Jeeves”</b>. I don’t know whether it was the fact it was written more than ten years later or that it was a full length story rather than many short stories but I actually found myself getting bored with the story. It wasn’t bad per se, it just wasn’t very good. There were long stretches where I thought the diction and dialogue could have used punching up and the whimsy that I had so enjoyed before was missing. There were several things I found offputting, not least of which was the continued reference to blackface and racist terms that not only severely date the book but also leave a sour taste after I’d finished the book. <br /><br />That’s not to say there weren’t enjoyable parts in the book. There were some great lines Bertie spouted off at random, echoing Wodehouse’s genius. <br /><br /><b>”He uttered a coarse expression which I wouldn't have thought he would have known. It just shows that you can bury yourself in the country and still somehow acquire a vocabulary. No doubt one picks up things from the neighbours -- the vicar, the local doctor, the man who brings the milk, and so on.”<br /><br />"I hadn't heard the door open, but the man was on the spot once more. My private belief, as I think I have mentioned before, is that Jeeves doesn't have to open doors. He's like one of those birds in India who bung their astral bodies about--the chaps, I mean, who having gone into thin air in Bombay, reassemble the parts and appear two minutes later in Calcutta. Only some such theory will account for the fact that he's not there one moment and is there the next. He just seems to float from Spot A to Spot B like some form of gas.”</b><br /><br />I think the main complaint and problem is the Jeeves-Wooster balance is missing. The best part of these books is the back and forth between Bertie and his valet. Usually Bertie’s done something foolish and Jeeves must reel him in to point out the error of his ways. For a large part of this book the two are separated. Granted Bertie sneaks over to ask Jeeves’ opinion on occasion but Bertie is mostly left to fend for himself, therefore creating a very bland story. It’s fries without ketchup, cake without icing, toast without jam, etc. The two of them work best together. I also found the fact that the entire plot hinged on the bajolele being a hated instrument very annoying. Surely there was another way to get Bertie out to the country without ditching Jeeves. Perhaps they could have disagreed with something after reaching the cottage?<br /><br />Ultimately I think I was spoiled by <b>”The Inimitable Jeeves”</b> which I previously reviewed. It was just better overall so if you are looking to read a Jeeves &amp; Wooster book I would suggest that one.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1771949379951454107" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
